From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Colorless gas with the formula NO

Not to be confused with [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide").

This article is about a molecule of one nitrogen atom and one oxygen atom. For other chemical combinations of nitrogen and oxygen, see [nitrogen oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide "Nitrogen oxide"). For the use of nitric oxide as a medication or in biology, see [Biological functions of nitric oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_functions_of_nitric_oxide "Biological functions of nitric oxide").

Nitric oxide [![Image 28: Skeletal formula of nitric oxide with bond length](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Nitric-oxide-2D.svg/250px-Nitric-oxide-2D.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nitric-oxide-2D.svg "Skeletal formula of nitric oxide with bond length")

![Image 46: ☒](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/20px-X_mark.svg.png)N[verify](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:ComparePages&rev1=477001381&page2=Nitric+oxide)([what is](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Chemicals/Chembox_validation "Wikipedia:WikiProject Chemicals/Chembox validation")![Image 47: check](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Yes_check.svg/20px-Yes_check.svg.png)Y![Image 48: ☒](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/X_mark.svg/20px-X_mark.svg.png)N?) 

[Infobox references](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References "Wikipedia:Chemical infobox")

Chemical compound

**Nitric oxide** (**nitrogen oxide**, **nitrogen monooxide**, or **nitrogen monoxide**[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Nomenclature_2005-1)) is a colorless gas with the formula **NO**. It is one of the principal [oxides of nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxides_of_nitrogen "Oxides of nitrogen"). Nitric oxide is a [free radical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_radical "Free radical"): it has an [unpaired electron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unpaired_electron "Unpaired electron"), which is sometimes denoted by a dot in its [chemical formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_formula "Chemical formula") (•N=O or •NO). Nitric oxide is also a [heteronuclear](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heteronuclear "Heteronuclear")[diatomic molecule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diatomic_molecule "Diatomic molecule"), a class of molecules whose study spawned early modern [theories of chemical bonding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_orbital_theory "Molecular orbital theory").[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-G&E-6)

An important [intermediate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reaction_intermediate "Reaction intermediate") in [industrial chemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_industry "Chemical industry"), nitric oxide forms in combustion systems and can be generated by lightning in thunderstorms. In mammals, including humans, nitric oxide is a [signaling molecule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Signaling_molecule "Signaling molecule") in many physiological and pathological processes.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-7) It was proclaimed the "[Molecule of the Year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecule_of_the_Year "Molecule of the Year")" in 1992.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-pmid1361684-8) The [1998 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Physiology_or_Medicine#1951.E2.80.932000 "List of Nobel laureates in Physiology or Medicine") was awarded for discovering nitric oxide's role as a cardiovascular signalling molecule.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-9) Its impact extends beyond biology, with applications in medicine, such as the development of [sildenafil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sildenafil "Sildenafil") (Viagra), and in industry, including [semiconductor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiconductor "Semiconductor") manufacturing.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-10)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-11)

Nitric oxide should not be confused with [nitrogen dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide "Nitrogen dioxide") (NO 2), a brown gas and major [air pollutant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_pollutant "Air pollutant"), or with [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide") (N 2 O), an [anesthetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_anaesthetic "General anaesthetic") gas.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-G&E-6)

History
-------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: History")]

Nitric oxide (NO) was first identified by [Joseph Priestley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Priestley "Joseph Priestley") in the late 18th century, originally seen as merely a toxic byproduct of combustion and an environmental pollutant.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-12) Its biological significance was later uncovered in the 1980s when researchers [Robert F. Furchgott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_F._Furchgott "Robert F. Furchgott"), [Louis J. Ignarro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Ignarro "Louis Ignarro"), and [Ferid Murad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferid_Murad "Ferid Murad") discovered its critical role as a [vasodilator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vasodilation "Vasodilation") in the cardiovascular system, a breakthrough that earned them the 1998 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-13)

Physical properties
-------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Physical properties")]

### Electronic configuration

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Electronic configuration")]

The ground-state [electronic configuration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_configuration "Electronic configuration") of NO in united-atom notation is[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-14)(1 σ)2(2 σ)2(3 σ)2(4 σ∗)2(5 σ)2(1 π)4(2 π∗)1.{\displaystyle (1\sigma )^{2}(2\sigma )^{2}(3\sigma )^{2}(4\sigma ^{*})^{2}(5\sigma )^{2}(1\pi )^{4}(2\pi ^{*})^{1}.}![Image 49: {\displaystyle (1\sigma )^{2}(2\sigma )^{2}(3\sigma )^{2}(4\sigma ^{*})^{2}(5\sigma )^{2}(1\pi )^{4}(2\pi ^{*})^{1}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b2e86226fc05a6ef5e83828ae29bff9dcbae4e4c) The first two [orbitals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_orbital "Molecular orbital") are actually pure atomic 1 _s_ O and 1 _s_ N from oxygen and nitrogen respectively and therefore are usually not noted in the united-atom notation. Orbitals noted with an asterisk are antibonding. The ordering of 5σ and 1π according to their binding energies is subject to discussion. Removal of a 1π electron leads to 6 states whose energies span over a range starting at a lower level than a 5σ electron an extending to a higher level. This is due to the different orbital momentum couplings between a 1π and a 2π electron.

The lone electron in the 2π orbital makes NO a doublet (_X_ 2 Π) in its ground state, whose degeneracy is split in the fine structure from [spin–orbit coupling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin%E2%80%93orbit_coupling "Spin–orbit coupling") with a total momentum _J_ = 3/2 or _J_ = 1/2.

### Dipole

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Dipole")]

The dipole of NO has been measured experimentally to 0.15740[D](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Debye "Debye") and is oriented from O to N (−NO+) due to the transfer of negative electronic charge from oxygen to nitrogen.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-15)

Reactions
---------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Reactions")]

### With di- and triatomic molecules

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: With di- and triatomic molecules")]

Upon condensing to a [neat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neat_(bartending) "Neat (bartending)") liquid, nitric oxide [dimerizes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry) "Dimer (chemistry)") to colorless [dinitrogen dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dinitrogen_dioxide "Dinitrogen dioxide") (O=N–N=O), but the association is weak and reversible. The N–N distance in crystalline NO is 218 pm, nearly twice the N–O distance. Condensation in a highly polar environment instead gives the red alternant isomer O=N–O+=N−.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-G&E-6)

Since the heat of formation of •NO is [endothermic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endothermic "Endothermic"), NO can be decomposed to the elements. [Catalytic converters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalytic_converter "Catalytic converter") in cars exploit this reaction:

2 •NO → O 2 + N 2
When exposed to [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), nitric oxide converts into [nitrogen dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide "Nitrogen dioxide"):

2 •NO + O 2 → 2 •NO 2
This reaction is thought to occur via the intermediates ONOO• and the red compound ONOONO.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Galliker_Kissner_Nauser_Koppenol_pp._6161%E2%80%936168-16)

In water, nitric oxide reacts with oxygen to form [nitrous acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_acid "Nitrous acid") (HNO 2). The reaction is thought to proceed via the following [stoichiometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stoichiometry "Stoichiometry"):

4 •NO + O 2 + 2 H 2 O → 4 HNO 2
Nitric oxide reacts with [fluorine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluorine "Fluorine"), [chlorine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chlorine "Chlorine"), and [bromine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bromine "Bromine") to form the nitrosyl halides, such as [nitrosyl chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrosyl_chloride "Nitrosyl chloride"):

2 •NO + Cl 2 → 2 NOCl
With NO 2, also a radical, NO combines to form the intensely blue [dinitrogen trioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dinitrogen_trioxide "Dinitrogen trioxide"):[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-G&E-6)

•NO + •NO 2 ⇌ ON−NO 2

### Organic chemistry

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: Organic chemistry")]

"Traube reaction" redirects here; not to be confused with [Traube purine synthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Traube_purine_synthesis "Traube purine synthesis").

Nitric oxide rarely sees organic chemistry use. Most reactions with it produce complex mixtures of salts, separable only through careful [recrystallization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recrystallization_(chemistry) "Recrystallization (chemistry)").[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Radicals-17)

The addition of a nitric oxide [moiety](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moiety_(chemistry) "Moiety (chemistry)") to another molecule is often referred to as _[nitrosylation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrosylation "Nitrosylation")_. The **Traube reaction** is the [addition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Addition_reaction "Addition reaction") of a two [equivalents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equivalent_(chemistry) "Equivalent (chemistry)") of nitric oxide onto an [enolate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enolate "Enolate"), giving a diazeniumdiolate (also called a _nitrosohydroxylamine_).[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-18) The product can undergo a subsequent retro-[aldol reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aldol_reaction "Aldol reaction"), giving an overall process similar to the [haloform reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloform_reaction "Haloform reaction"). For example, nitric oxide reacts with [acetone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acetone "Acetone") and an [alkoxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alkoxide "Alkoxide") to form a diazeniumdiolate on each [α position](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_carbon "Alpha carbon"), with subsequent loss of [methyl acetate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methyl_acetate "Methyl acetate") as a [by-product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/By-product "By-product"):[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-19)

[![Image 50: Traube reaction](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/TraubeReaction.svg/500px-TraubeReaction.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:TraubeReaction.svg "Traube reaction")
This reaction, which was discovered around 1898, remains of interest in nitric oxide [prodrug](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prodrug "Prodrug") research. Nitric oxide can also react directly with [sodium methoxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_methoxide "Sodium methoxide"), ultimately forming [sodium formate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_formate "Sodium formate") and [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide") by way of an _N_-methoxydiazeniumdiolate.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-20)

Sufficiently basic [secondary amines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_amine "Secondary amine") undergo a Traube-like reaction to give [NONOates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NONOate "NONOate").[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-21) However, very few nucleophiles undergo the Traube reaction, either failing to adduce NO or immediately decomposing with [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide") release.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Radicals-17)

### Coordination complexes

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Coordination complexes")]

Main article: [Metal nitrosyl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metal_nitrosyl "Metal nitrosyl")

Nitric oxide reacts with [transition metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transition_metal "Transition metal") to give complexes called [metal nitrosyls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metal_nitrosyl "Metal nitrosyl"). The most common bonding mode of nitric oxide is the terminal linear type (M−NO).[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-G&E-6) Alternatively, nitric oxide can serve as a one-electron pseudohalide. In such complexes, the M−N−O group is characterized by an angle between 120° and 140°. The NO group can also bridge between metal centers through the nitrogen atom in a variety of geometries.

Production and preparation
--------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Production and preparation")]

In commercial settings, nitric oxide is produced by the [oxidation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidation "Oxidation") of [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia") at 750–900°C (normally at 850°C) with [platinum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platinum "Platinum") as [catalyst](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalyst "Catalyst") in the [Ostwald process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ostwald_process "Ostwald process"):

4 NH 3 + 5 O 2 → 4 •NO + 6 H 2 O
The uncatalyzed [endothermic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endothermic "Endothermic") reaction of [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") (O 2) and [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen") (N 2), which is effected at high temperature (>2000°C) by lightning has not been developed into a practical commercial synthesis (see [Birkeland–Eyde process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birkeland%E2%80%93Eyde_process "Birkeland–Eyde process")):

N 2 + O 2 → 2 •NO

### Laboratory methods

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Laboratory methods")]

In the laboratory, nitric oxide is conveniently generated by reduction of dilute [nitric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_acid "Nitric acid") with [copper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copper "Copper"):

8 HNO 3 + 3 Cu → 3 Cu(NO 3)2 + 4 H 2 O + 2 •NO
An alternative route involves the reduction of nitrous acid in the form of [sodium nitrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_nitrite "Sodium nitrite") or [potassium nitrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium_nitrite "Potassium nitrite"):

2 NaNO 2 + 2 NaI + 2 H 2 SO 4 → I 2 + 2 Na 2 SO 4 + 2 H 2 O + 2 •NO 2 NaNO 2 + 2 FeSO 4 + 3 H 2 SO 4 → Fe 2(SO 4)3 + 2 NaHSO 4 + 2 H 2 O + 2 •NO 3 KNO 2 + KNO 3 + Cr 2 O 3 → 2 K 2 CrO 4 + 4 •NO
The iron(II) sulfate route is simple and has been used in undergraduate laboratory experiments.

So-called [NONOate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NONOate "NONOate") compounds are also used for nitric oxide generation, especially in biological laboratories. However, other Traube adducts may decompose to instead give [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide").[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-22)

Detection and assay
-------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Detection and assay")]

[![Image 51](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_%28NO%29_%28white%29_in_the_cytoplasm_%28green%29_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg/250px-The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_%28NO%29_%28white%29_in_the_cytoplasm_%28green%29_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_(NO)_(white)_in_the_cytoplasm_(green)_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg)

Nitric oxide (white) in [conifer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pinophyta "Pinophyta") cells, visualized using DAF-2 DA (diaminofluorescein diacetate)

Nitric oxide concentration can be determined using a [chemiluminescent reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemiluminescence "Chemiluminescence") involving [ozone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone "Ozone").[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-23) A sample containing nitric oxide is mixed with a large quantity of ozone. The nitric oxide reacts with the ozone to produce [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") and [nitrogen dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide "Nitrogen dioxide"), accompanied with emission of [light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light "Light") ([chemiluminescence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemiluminescence "Chemiluminescence")):

•NO + O 3 → •NO 2 + O 2 + _hν_
which can be measured with a [photodetector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photodetector "Photodetector"). The amount of light produced is proportional to the amount of nitric oxide in the sample.

Other methods of testing include [electroanalysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrochemistry "Electrochemistry") (amperometric approach), where ·NO reacts with an electrode to induce a current or voltage change. The detection of NO radicals in biological tissues is particularly difficult due to the short lifetime and concentration of these radicals in tissues. One of the few practical methods is [spin trapping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_trapping "Spin trapping") of nitric oxide with iron-[dithiocarbamate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dithiocarbamate "Dithiocarbamate") complexes and subsequent detection of the mono-nitrosyl-iron complex with [electron paramagnetic resonance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_paramagnetic_resonance "Electron paramagnetic resonance") (EPR).[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-24)[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-25)

A group of [fluorescent dye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluorescent_dye "Fluorescent dye") indicators that are also available in [acetylated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acetyl "Acetyl") form for intracellular measurements exist. The most common compound is [4,5-diaminofluorescein](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=4,5-diaminofluorescein&action=edit&redlink=1 "4,5-diaminofluorescein (page does not exist)") (DAF-2).[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-undefined-26)

Environmental effects
---------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Environmental effects")]

Main article: [NOx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NOx "NOx")

### Acid rain deposition

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Acid rain deposition")]

Nitric oxide reacts with the [hydroperoxyl radical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroperoxyl "Hydroperoxyl") (HO•

2) to form nitrogen dioxide (NO 2), which then can react with a [hydroxyl radical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical "Hydroxyl radical") (HO•) to produce [nitric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_acid "Nitric acid") (HNO 3):

•NO + HO•

2 → •NO 2 + HO••NO 2 + HO• → HNO 3
Nitric acid, along with [sulfuric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfuric_acid "Sulfuric acid"), contributes to [acid rain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_rain "Acid rain") deposition.

### Ozone depletion

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Ozone depletion")]

•NO participates in [ozone layer depletion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone_layer_depletion "Ozone layer depletion"). Nitric oxide reacts with stratospheric [ozone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone "Ozone") to form O 2 and nitrogen dioxide:

•NO + O 3 → •NO 2 + O 2
This reaction is also utilized to measure concentrations of •NO in control volumes.

### Precursor to NO 2

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Precursor to NO2")]

As seen in the [acid deposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#Acid_rain_deposition) section, nitric oxide can transform into nitrogen dioxide (this can happen with the hydroperoxy radical, HO•

2, or diatomic oxygen, O 2). Symptoms of short-term nitrogen dioxide exposure include nausea, [dyspnea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dyspnea "Dyspnea") and headache. Long-term effects could include impaired immune and [respiratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Respiratory_system "Respiratory system") function.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-27)

Biological functions
--------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Biological functions")]

Main article: [Biological functions of nitric oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_functions_of_nitric_oxide "Biological functions of nitric oxide")

NO is a [gaseous signaling molecule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaseous_signaling_molecule "Gaseous signaling molecule").[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-28) It is a key [vertebrate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertebrate "Vertebrate")[biological messenger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Signal_transduction "Signal transduction"), playing a role in a variety of biological processes.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-29) It is a bioproduct in almost all types of organisms, including bacteria, plants, fungi, and animal cells.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-30)

Nitric oxide, an [endothelium-derived relaxing factor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endothelium-derived_relaxing_factor "Endothelium-derived relaxing factor") (EDRF), is biosynthesized endogenously from [L-arginine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arginine "Arginine"), [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), and [NADPH](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NADPH "NADPH") by various [nitric oxide synthase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide_synthase "Nitric oxide synthase") (NOS) [enzymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enzyme "Enzyme").[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Perez_2598%E2%80%932607.e1-31) Reduction of inorganic nitrate may also make nitric oxide.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-stryer-32) One of the main enzymatic targets of nitric oxide is [guanylyl cyclase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guanylate_cyclase "Guanylate cyclase").[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Hancock-2010-33) The binding of nitric oxide to the [heme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heme "Heme") region of the enzyme leads to activation, in the presence of iron.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Hancock-2010-33) Nitric oxide is highly reactive (having a lifetime of a few seconds), yet diffuses freely across membranes. These attributes make nitric oxide ideal for a transient [paracrine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paracrine "Paracrine") (between adjacent cells) and [autocrine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocrine "Autocrine") (within a single cell) signaling molecule.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-stryer-32) Once nitric oxide is converted to nitrates and nitrites by oxygen and water, cell signaling is deactivated.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Hancock-2010-33)

The [endothelium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endothelium "Endothelium") (inner lining) of [blood vessels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_vessel "Blood vessel") uses nitric oxide to signal the surrounding [smooth muscle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smooth_muscle "Smooth muscle") to relax, resulting in [vasodilation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vasodilation "Vasodilation") and increasing blood flow.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-stryer-32)[Sildenafil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sildenafil "Sildenafil") (Viagra) is a drug that uses the nitric oxide pathway. Sildenafil does not produce nitric oxide, but enhances the signals that are downstream of the nitric oxide pathway by protecting [cyclic guanosine monophosphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclic_guanosine_monophosphate "Cyclic guanosine monophosphate") (cGMP) from degradation by [cGMP-specific phosphodiesterase type 5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CGMP-specific_phosphodiesterase_type_5 "CGMP-specific phosphodiesterase type 5") (PDE5) in the [corpus cavernosum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corpus_cavernosum_penis "Corpus cavernosum penis"), allowing for the signal to be enhanced, and thus [vasodilation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vasodilation "Vasodilation").[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Perez_2598%E2%80%932607.e1-31) Another endogenous gaseous transmitter, [hydrogen sulfide (H 2 S)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide "Hydrogen sulfide") works with NO to induce vasodilation and angiogenesis in a cooperative manner.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-34)[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-Altaany_879%E2%80%93888-35)

Nasal breathing produces higher levels of exhaled nitric oxide compared to [oral breathing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mouth_breathing "Mouth breathing").[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-36)[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-37)

Occupational safety and health
------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Occupational safety and health")]

In the U.S., the [Occupational Safety and Health Administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration "Occupational Safety and Health Administration") (OSHA) has set the legal limit ([permissible exposure limit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit "Permissible exposure limit")) for nitric oxide exposure in the workplace as 25 ppm (30 mg/m 3) over an 8-hour workday. The [National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health "National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health") (NIOSH) has set a [recommended exposure limit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit "Recommended exposure limit") (REL) of 25 ppm (30 mg/m 3) over an 8-hour workday. At levels of 100 ppm, nitric oxide is [immediately dangerous to life and health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IDLH "IDLH").[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-38)

Explosion hazard
----------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Explosion hazard")]

Liquid nitrogen oxide is very sensitive to detonation even in the absence of fuel, and can be initiated as readily as nitroglycerin. Detonation of the endothermic liquid oxide close to its boiling point (−152°C or −241.6°F or 121.1 K) generated a 100 kbar pulse and fragmented the test equipment. It is the simplest molecule that is capable of detonation in all three phases. The liquid oxide is sensitive and may explode during distillation, and this has been the cause of industrial accidents.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-39) Gaseous nitric oxide detonates at about 2,300 metres per second (8,300 km/h; 5,100 mph), but as a solid it can reach a detonation velocity of 6,100 metres per second (22,000 km/h; 13,600 mph).[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_oxide#cite_note-40)
